    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_5c4f7844-da6a-4611-a632-f3b77baafedd'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer10740596826691932472">Tertuliano Maximo Afonso is a history teacher in a secondary school. He is divorced, involved in a rather one-sided relationship with a bank clerk, and he is depressed. To lift his depression, a colleague suggests he rent a certain video. Tertuliano watches the film and is unimpressed. During the night, noises in his apartment wake him. He goes into the living room to find</span>
  <span id="freeText10740596826691932472" style="display:none">Tertuliano Maximo Afonso is a history teacher in a secondary school. He is divorced, involved in a rather one-sided relationship with a bank clerk, and he is depressed. To lift his depression, a colleague suggests he rent a certain video. Tertuliano watches the film and is unimpressed. During the night, noises in his apartment wake him. He goes into the living room to find that the VCR is replaying the video, and as he watches in astonishment, he sees an actor who looks exactly like him - or, more specifically, exactly like the man he was five years before, moustachioed and fuller in the face. He sleeps badly.<br /><br />Against his own better judgement, Tertuliano decides to pursue his double. As he establishes the man's identity, what begins as a whimsical story becomes a dark meditation on identity and, perhaps, on the crass assumptions behind cloning - that we are merely our outward appearance rather than the sum of our experiences.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10740596826691932472" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='communityQuestionSubTitle'>
Popular Answered Questions

<div id='communityQuestionsWithAnswers'>
<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/438037-what-is-with-this-guy-and-not-using">What is with this guy and not using periods? Just pages and pages of commas and run on sentences. Makes it hard to follow the dialogue at times. Anyone know what this is about? Or, does it get easier to stand as you read on?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_438037'><a id="like_count_community_question_438037" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/438037?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">7 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F54120.The_Double">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/438037-what-is-with-this-guy-and-not-using">5 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/438037-what-is-with-this-guy-and-not-using">See all 6 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/10258000-eduardo-a"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_100x100-c7bfe730633c9983e27caaa66a8e244a.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/10258000-eduardo-a">Eduardo A.</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Saramago has explained this earlier: <br/><br/>«As you know, when we speak, we don't use punctuation. We pause [to breath] and even, as I say in my books, the o<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Saramago has explained this earlier: <br /><br />«As you know, when we speak, we don't use punctuation. We pause [to breath] and even, as I say in my books, the only two punctuation marks are the full stop (or period, in American English) and the comma, are not punctuation marks, they are a pause, a brief pause and a long pause. In the end, as I often say, to speak is to compose music» - in Expresso, 2004.<br /><br />«(..) I see myself as an oral narrator when I write and that the words by me written ought to be read as well as to be heard. Now, the oral narrator doesn't need punctuation, speaks as he was composing music and uses the same elements as a musician: sounds and pauses, highs and lows, some, brief or long, others.» - in Cadernos de Lanzarote – Diário II (1994) <br /><br />I translated it as best as I could from the following link: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://ciberduvidas.iscte-iul.pt/artigos/categorias/idioma/saramago-o-escritor-que-brinca-com-a-pontuacao/1691">https://ciberduvidas.iscte-iul.pt/art...</a><a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/758445-will-the-book-still-be-enjoyable-if-i-see">Will the book still be enjoyable if I see the movie?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_758445'><a id="like_count_community_question_758445" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/758445?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F54120.The_Double">like</a></span>

<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/19310513-justin"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1412604936i/19310513._UX100_CR0,26,100,100_.jpg" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/19310513-justin">Justin</a>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
It depends on your tastes. The book is very different from the movie, so there's no reason you need to limit yourself to one or the other.
</span>

      </span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099461654" href="/book/show/826799.The_Double">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=spain-portugal">spain-portugal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=postmodern-fiction">postmodern-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=nobel-laureates">nobel-laureates</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2339063891" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9025504931071310171">The thought of having a doppelgänger freaks me out, if I had one, I would rather not know. Even somebody bearing a strong resemblance to one's self is weird enough, but an actual replica? with the same moles, the same scars, and the same voice. No thanks. I am me, and I want to keep it that way. If only History professor Tertuliano Máximo Afonso returned the video tape to the shop and forgot all about what he witnessed on screen. But he couldn't, a curiosity built up inside him. Just who is that</span>
  <span id="freeText9025504931071310171" style="display:none">The thought of having a doppelgänger freaks me out, if I had one, I would rather not know. Even somebody bearing a strong resemblance to one's self is weird enough, but an actual replica? with the same moles, the same scars, and the same voice. No thanks. I am me, and I want to keep it that way. If only History professor Tertuliano Máximo Afonso returned the video tape to the shop and forgot all about what he witnessed on screen. But he couldn't, a curiosity built up inside him. Just who is that man who looks like my identical twin? I want to know who he is. I must find him.<br /><br />Made in our own image, the double can incarnate our evil deeds, or kindly take our place in order to perform a seemingly impossible task, it is one of the most complex and richly told fables. José Saramago is clearly influenced by the old tales. Whether a different version of Christ's Passion, or an account of the universal scourge of being sightless, Saramago has found in these primordial narratives fodder for his own fictional world. One I would say he excels at. The main character, Tertuliano, becomes obsessed with the idea of meeting the person he takes to be his double, a bit part actor called Antonio Claro. After obsessively viewing dozens of other movies by the same production company, he gets results by discovering his name. And ponders on his next move. This would lead to a chain of events that not only desn't go according to plan, but opens up a whole other set of possibilities that spells danger for the people he most cares about, including his girlfriend, Maria da Paz.<br /><br />I loved Saramago's story, the way it delved deep into my consciousness, resulting in a finale that sat well with me. As for Tertuliano himself, a guy who meandered through life feeling like someone owed him a favour. He believes he is depressed. No. He wants to be depressed. Why? A nice girl who is far too good for him, a decent well payed job. Put the violin away. To me he just appeared to be a bit of a drip. The long continuous sentences and detailed faintly comic descriptions of Tertuliano's comings and goings started to annoy me, and then it didn't. Saramago may be a Nobel laureate who feels obliged to write like a Prose experimentalist filling the pages with hardly any paragraph breaks, but thankfully, from my own perspective this is something that wasn't new, and writing style eventually I came to truly admire. After the point of no return, when the Identicals finally meet, I loved every minute right through to it's conclusion, it just took a while to get into the story.<br /><br />There's nothing particularly new in positing a logical world and then introducing an absurd element which leads to an unravelling of identity. It's true that he takes the story in some unexpected directions, but that's to be expected, it keeps you on your toes. His history teacher becomes more decisive, more alert to the world around him, more committed to his life in the shadow of his doppelgänger. His depression is a thing of the past. He simply hasn't the mind anymore to wallow in pity knowing he's out there. The actor Antonio Claro starts off as the innocent party only to grow more into a menace the longer the story progresses. I had absolutely no idea what was likely to happen from one moment to the next. Isn't that such a wondrous feeling to possess whilst reading a novel. <br /><br />One of the other pleasures of the book is the way Saramago faithfully follows the relentless logic of the situation he has devised. When the two men meet, it is at once apparent that they are indeed doubles. Down to the moles on their forearms and the date of their birth. Immediately, existential questions are raised. Which of them, Tertuliano worriedly wonders, is the original and which the duplicate. No answers there. When it turns out that Claro was born a half-hour before Tertuliano, it somehow becomes inevitable that one of them will have to be destroyed. Having been born on the same day, they both want to know, of course, if they are destined also to die on the same day.<br /><br />Saramago doesn’t bother with quotation marks to denote when people are speaking, and he doesn’t break up conversations aside from inserting commas in between each person’s statements, but that aside I found it great, Saramago on top form. He certainly wasn't a writer shy of confidence within himself, and he maybe one of the masters of the Long Sentence to the annoyance of some, but from a psychological and moral standpoint he simply sucked me in.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9025504931071310171" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16336935946827262722" style="display:none"><b>Quantum Physics of Identity</b><br /><br /><i>"Only a common sense with the imagination of a poet could have invented the wheel."</i> Not a bad self-referential summary of Saramago's The Double. <br /><br />This book is common sense and imagination applied to human identity and the result is a literary wheel turning round and round in the minds of two apparently identical individuals. How random is life anyway? <br /><br />Once again Saramago creates a situation in which to explore the oddness of being alive and knowing it.</span>

  <span id="freeText8270153612031579426" style="display:none">...Infuriating!<br /><br />Incredibly hard to read! This was a self-inflicted punishment by an insipid reader trying to introduce himself to a compulsively revolting writer!<br /><br />Saramago, who is, like his name suggests, a "mago", or wizard, pretty much singlehandly diminishes the joy that reading brings, making it all one self-assured, self-serving CHORE.<br /><br />Man, I hated this book. The story could be a novella, would be an amazing and silly and cool one at that, but in novel form, well... all Saramago does is throw words &amp; strips &amp; strings of consciousness-- all very art noveau. But is this a contribution to our literary canon? It is a step backward...<br /><br />The protagonist is boring, has a boring life, has boring things happen to him. The plot is intriguing to just say something nice about it: this man finds his double. The double, a figure just as unlikable as its model, then tries to live the protagonist's life. It ends at a terrible point... the dark ambiguity wins! Yipee! See, am an expert at finding the silver lining.<br /><br />I recommend this solely to my enemies. It's that lame.</span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099461654" href="/book/show/826799.The_Double">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=read-2016">read-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=modern-classics">modern-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32318315-hugh?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>,

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1647328842" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3939591516523549573">Saramago was a unique literary magician, and this is one of his most hauntingly memorable books. This one is a real slow burner, which demands great patience of its reader, but like so many of Saramago's books it is full of dry humour. <br/><br/>The first part of the book might even be described as dull, as the long and apparently rambling sentences, conversations without quotes, and occasional asides from his omniscient narrator set up a picture of an unsympathetic and drab antihero, a depressed history </span>
  <span id="freeText3939591516523549573" style="display:none">Saramago was a unique literary magician, and this is one of his most hauntingly memorable books. This one is a real slow burner, which demands great patience of its reader, but like so many of Saramago's books it is full of dry humour. <br /><br />The first part of the book might even be described as dull, as the long and apparently rambling sentences, conversations without quotes, and occasional asides from his omniscient narrator set up a picture of an unsympathetic and drab antihero, a depressed history teacher who watches a video recommended by a colleague and sees a bit part actor who is his exact likeness. <br /><br />As in his modern parables <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2526.Blindness" title="Blindness by José Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">Blindness</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/47667.Seeing" title="Seeing by José Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">Seeing</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1456607.Death_at_Intervals" title="Death at Intervals by José Saramago" rel="nofollow noopener">Death at Intervals</a>, Saramago takes an implausible scenario and slowly and inexorably explores the consequences - this one is a more personal story.<br /><br />The last 100 pages or so are absolutely gripping and the denouement is very clever - many readers may not get that far, but those who do may find the book unforgettable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3939591516523549573" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3767316" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3767316" />
    <a title="Brenda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/149902-brenda"><img alt="Brenda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1182695644p2/149902.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3767316?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brenda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brenda" href="/user/show/149902-brenda">Brenda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156032582" href="/book/show/78188.The_Double">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Saramago fans</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3767316" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13452735254854416442">I should preface this review with a disclaimer that I both love Jose Saramago and magical realism. Adore both, actually. Do not judge my nerdiness. I will loudly and proudly shout to the line behind me at the book store that I cannot wait to read about a magical mountain range. And when the manager has to come over and escort me to the door with my purchases, I'll shout even louder about how much I admire the tale of an old village woman who lived to be 250. And when the manager tells me I canno</span>
  <span id="freeText13452735254854416442" style="display:none">I should preface this review with a disclaimer that I both love Jose Saramago and magical realism. Adore both, actually. Do not judge my nerdiness. I will loudly and proudly shout to the line behind me at the book store that I cannot wait to read about a magical mountain range. And when the manager has to come over and escort me to the door with my purchases, I'll shout even louder about how much I admire the tale of an old village woman who lived to be 250. And when the manager tells me I cannot come back, well then I'll probably just go to my car, because what a jerk.<br /><br />Like all of Saramago's books, this one starts with a puzzling anomaly in the normal, regimented, and dull life of an unassuming nobody living in a large, identity-robbing city. Then the next two-thirds of the book provide extremely detailed subtleties into the nature and personality of the protagonist and his environment. This section is so beautifully written, but it almost seems like he loses the story. Until bango! The last, gripping 75 pages or so where the entire plot erupts into action sequences and discoveries. What is so brilliant about Saramago is he does not spell anything out directly. You need to really absorb the first sections of the book to figure out the full meaning and unravel the plot.<br /><br />In this book, not only was the ending fast paced, it was also murderous and malicious and filled with grief and sex. More explicit than I anticipated and keeping me up late at night with my lamp burning. Loved it. And the translator must be a genius with words because translating a book where you have to dig up "persipacity" is cuckoo bananas. I highly recommend "The Double" if you like Saramago. If you generally don't care for his stuff, then no way will you like this one. At least that's my prediction because it's 100% his style the whole way through.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13452735254854416442" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <a title="Zach" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/428808-zach"><img alt="Zach" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536687522p2/428808.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/138123635?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zach" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zach" href="/user/show/428808-zach">Zach</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer138123635" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7257433981596673805">Well, I had planned on starting this review with some choice zingers about Saramago shameless burgling the plot of any or every Murakami novel, wherein some workaday shlub, with a boring job and a boring life and crushingly alone in the world, has a chance-or-not-chance run-in with some inexplicable unnatural or supernatural phenomenon which comes to consume him, only with the Portuguese author applying his trademark run-on sentences and incredibly lengthy paragraphs and narratological excursion</span>
  <span id="freeText7257433981596673805" style="display:none">Well, I had planned on starting this review with some choice zingers about Saramago shameless burgling the plot of any or every Murakami novel, wherein some workaday shlub, with a boring job and a boring life and crushingly alone in the world, has a chance-or-not-chance run-in with some inexplicable unnatural or supernatural phenomenon which comes to consume him, only with the Portuguese author applying his trademark run-on sentences and incredibly lengthy paragraphs and narratological excursions and un-demarcated dialogue, and replacing the Japanese author's focus on Jazz and Food with a focus on History and Philosophy, proceeding from which I planned on working through the copious notes that I took regarding the confusion of identity and the importance of words and narration in the construction of the self to discuss the existential dilemma at the heart of this novel and to solve what I thought would be a series of puzzles and confusions, but then, after hundreds of pages, I realized that this book was actually rather boring, and that Saramago wasn't saying anything that I found to be particularly interesting, and that the actions and choices of the main character were, frankly, inexplicable and unbelievable, and that the climax of the narrative was basically the most juvenile and unimaginative and misogynistic expression of conflict by which each of the doubles could supposedly strike deep at the heart of the other man's identity or individuality or manhood or whatever, and so I lost interest in investing that much work in this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7257433981596673805" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_466870294" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/466870294" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6971227-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564001692p2/6971227.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/466870294?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/6971227-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6971227-david?shelf=nobel-winners">nobel-winners</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6971227-david?shelf=latin-hispanic">latin-hispanic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer466870294" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16376569033022706691">I did not really enjoy Saramago's <i>The Double</i>. To begin with, it's a sloppy handling of a theme which has been done over-and-over, and better done at that: Dickin's <i>The Tale of Two Cities</i>, Poe's "William Wilson," Nabokov's <i>Despair</i> and Dostoyevsky's <i>The Double</i> were all better handlings of the doppelgänger theme than this, I felt. This felt kind of like a more sinister "The Parent Trap" or dropped episode of <i>The Twilight Zone</i> (dropped for being too long maybe). It wasn't bad, I won't say that. Ever</span>
  <span id="freeText16376569033022706691" style="display:none">I did not really enjoy Saramago's <i>The Double</i>. To begin with, it's a sloppy handling of a theme which has been done over-and-over, and better done at that: Dickin's <i>The Tale of Two Cities</i>, Poe's "William Wilson," Nabokov's <i>Despair</i> and Dostoyevsky's <i>The Double</i> were all better handlings of the doppelgänger theme than this, I felt. This felt kind of like a more sinister "The Parent Trap" or dropped episode of <i>The Twilight Zone</i> (dropped for being too long maybe). It wasn't bad, I won't say that. Everything was there: identity, doubles, blunt bludgeoning of routine, life and death, ideals and jealousies, etc. But at the end of the book I just felt that it was marginal: vain attempts at humor, tedious longeurs, unidimensional supporting characters. This might have made a fun, quirky novella, but as a full novel it hits a flat note.<br /><br />The issue of identity is brought into the twenty-first century, Tertuliano Maximo Afonso feels depressed and useless, and so decides to watch some movies to cure his ennui. He has a girlfriend, Maria da Paz, who he kind of likes, and a job which he kind of cares about. While watching the movies he notices that one of the extras in the movie is his exact double! His life suddenly takes on meaning to solve the mystery of his exact look-alike, he finds his name and ultimately confronts him. They briefly switch lives, and tell each other a bit about themselves. They have the same birthday, but the double (claims he) was born before Tertuliano, which Tertuliano takes to mean that he is the copy, the supernumerary. <blockquote>
  <i>It was said that one of them, either the actor or the history teacher, was superfluous in this world, but you weren't, you weren't superfluous, there is no duplicate of you to come and replace you at your mother's side, you were unique, just as every ordinary person is unique, truly unique.</i>
</blockquote><br />Until the confrontation, the book feels a but lifeless: only Tertuliano has any interest for the reader, even Maria seems to be only a silhouette populating the stage of shadows which is <i>The Double</i>. But the climax cannot hold, it falls away fast after their initial inspection to confirm their identicalness (same height: check, same build: check, same dick: check, etc.). <i>The Double</i> read like a story which came to Saramago as a scene (Faulkner notoriously envisioned all his books as one scene, and the book grew around that scene/image), the scene of confrontation, but the rest, the sprawl, feels sloppily done. The prose is well done in many sections, though awkward in others, but the story feels like a dwarf trying to fill a giant's suit: there is too much excess, not enough substance. The characters are too small for the world they populate, and the story is too narrow for the binding.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16376569033022706691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_6733883" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6733883" />
    <a title="Ama" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83435-ama"><img alt="Ama" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1182286732p2/83435.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6733883?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ama" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ama" href="/user/show/83435-ama">Ama</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people who're fond of magical realism</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6733883" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer452313148785385841">I figured out the inevitable ending of this book about halfway through it, but getting to the end was certainly not tedious . The language (lyrically translated by Margaret Costa) is full of unusual but vivid imagery ("...she had noticed a kind of embarassed catch in his voice, a disharmony that occasionally distorted his delivery, like the characteristic vibrato produced by a cracked water jar when struck with the knuckles"), and the novel is filled with sophisticated phrases that fit oddly wel</span>
  <span id="freeText452313148785385841" style="display:none">I figured out the inevitable ending of this book about halfway through it, but getting to the end was certainly not tedious . The language (lyrically translated by Margaret Costa) is full of unusual but vivid imagery ("...she had noticed a kind of embarassed catch in his voice, a disharmony that occasionally distorted his delivery, like the characteristic vibrato produced by a cracked water jar when struck with the knuckles"), and the novel is filled with sophisticated phrases that fit oddly well with the scrupulously average protagonist without feeling false or forced. Even the author's tendency to combine what ought to be three pages of dialogue into one run-on sentence (strung along by obsessively linked commas) feels natural and comprehensible after the first few instances. <br />Though the plot may feel slightly hackneyed at first glance('The Prince and the Pauper' goes awry)the brilliance of the execution saves it from any such accusation overall. This is a wonderful and diverting choice for anyone willing to engage in the slight relinquishment of common sense that this novel (and the genre) requires.</span>

<div id="review_3519643386" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3519643386" />
    <a title="Denisse" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8759700-denisse"><img alt="Denisse" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1606102662p2/8759700.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3519643386?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Denisse" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Denisse" href="/user/show/8759700-denisse">Denisse</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788490628751" href="/book/show/25922886-el-hombre-duplicado">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3519643386" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4251351596775105090">¡Damn! I love open endings. I've only read one more Saramago's and I can say he can achieve both the long and deep thoughts as much as the unbridled suspense. The way he destroys the peaceful normality of our two main characters is almost wicked. And still, who wouldn't lose the mind when knowing we are not unique, that our existence might be a mistake? <b>The absence of punctuation marks can be bothering at first but once you get into the story, that fades away and you'd be left alone with a premi</b></span>
  <span id="freeText4251351596775105090" style="display:none">¡Damn! I love open endings. I've only read one more Saramago's and I can say he can achieve both the long and deep thoughts as much as the unbridled suspense. The way he destroys the peaceful normality of our two main characters is almost wicked. And still, who wouldn't lose the mind when knowing we are not unique, that our existence might be a mistake? <b>The absence of punctuation marks can be bothering at first but once you get into the story, that fades away and you'd be left alone with a premise that is both mind-blowing and catastrophic.</b> The author places a stage where he is one more character in the story, perhaps the most important one.<br /><br /><br /><b>
  <i> “Quien es este, y ni más de cuatro o cinco millones de años conseguirán probablemente dar respuesta.” </i>
</b><br /><br />La temática del doble no es nada nueva y no tengo tantos puntos de comparación, pero si que Saramago encuentra una forma de hacerte pensar mientras te mantiene leyendo aprisa, es impresionante lo rápido que pasa de la exposición al suspenso. <b>Construye su libro mientras destruye a sus personajes. </b><br /><br />El inicio puede ser algo tambaleante, al igual que nuestro protagonista al darse cuenta de su doble y como esto lo lleva a hacerse las preguntas del millón, ¿quién es el original? <b> ¿soy un error? </b> Toda la trama gira entorno a ellas. El autor entra en escena tomando por momentos el protagónico para comentar las acciones de Tertuliano y Antonio y esa casi puesta en escena siempre me gusto. Dejaba una sensación extraña, pero en el buen sentido de la palabra. <br /><br />Personalmente me encanto la historia y la conversación que puede generar la lectura. Para mi no hay nada como un libro que te atrapa y te hace pensar al mismo tiempo. El final es impactante, es mi tipo de final favorito. <b>Un cierre perfecto.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="4251351596775105090" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_139969141" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/139969141" />
    <a title="Madeline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/111921-madeline"><img alt="Madeline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1220803522p2/111921.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/139969141?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Madeline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Madeline" href="/user/show/111921-madeline">Madeline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=the-list">the-list</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer139969141" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11070634863553544080">"The man who has just come into the shop to rent a video bears on his identity card a most unusual name, a name with a classical flavor that time has staled, neither more nor less than Tertuliano Maximo Afonso. The Maximo and the Afonso, which are in more common usage, he can just about tolerate, depending, of course, on the mood he's in, but the Tertuliano weighs on him like a gravestone and has done, ever since he first realized that the wretched name lent itself to being spoken in an ironic, </span>
  <span id="freeText11070634863553544080" style="display:none">"The man who has just come into the shop to rent a video bears on his identity card a most unusual name, a name with a classical flavor that time has staled, neither more nor less than Tertuliano Maximo Afonso. The Maximo and the Afonso, which are in more common usage, he can just about tolerate, depending, of course, on the mood he's in, but the Tertuliano weighs on him like a gravestone and has done, ever since he first realized that the wretched name lent itself to being spoken in an ironic, potentially offensive tone. He is a history teacher at a secondary school, and a colleague had suggested the video to him with the warning, It's not exactly a masterpiece of cinema, but it might keep you amused for an hour and a half. Tertuliano Maximo Afonso is greatly in need of stimuli to distract him, he lives alone and gets bored, or, to speak with the clinical exactitude that the present day requires, he has succumbed to the temporary weakness of spirit known as depression." <br /><br />A very interesting premise, written in the overly-analytical and run-on way that Saramago is so fond of: as described above, a history teacher with a funny name watches a video, and to his shock discovers that one of the extras in the film looks exactly like him. He becomes obsessed with this apparent identical twin, and eventually tracks the man down and contacts him. From then on, both are obsessed with the idea of why they are identical, down to their moles and scars, and which one is the copy of the other. Disaster follows, and even though I was able to guess the disaster, it was still interesting. <br /><br />But I'm only giving it three stars, firstly because I'm frankly getting a little tired of Jose Saramgo's rambling writing style. Would it really ruin the story that much if he put in some goddamn quotation marks around the dialogue every now and then? It's a stylistic choice, <i>I get it</i>. Also, I have a sneaking suspicion that Saramago doesn't like women - if the representation of female characters in this book and <i>Blindness</i> (the other book of his that I've read), he certainly doesn't find them interesting enough to exert much influence on a story. Also the ending of this particular book is vaguely misogynist and deeply unpleasant in regards to the female characters, and I did not care for it at all. <br /><br />Honestly, I think this story would have actually worked best as a 30-minute episode of <i>The Twilight Zone</i>. <br /><br />"We all know that each day that dawns is the first for some, and will be the last for others, and that for most people it will be just another day. For the history teacher Tertuliano Maximo Afonso, this day in which we find ourselves, in which we continue to exist, since there is no reason to believe it will be our last, will not be just another day. One might say that it appeared in the world with the possibility of being another first day, another beginning, and indicating, therefore, another destiny. Everything depends on what steps Tertuliano Maximo Afonso takes today. However, the procession, as people used to say in times gone by, is just about to leave the church. Let's follow it."</span>
  <a data-text-id="11070634863553544080" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_249609480" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/249609480" />
    <a title="Tej" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5074564-tej"><img alt="Tej" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1460673152p2/5074564.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/249609480?book_show_action=true">Dec 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tej" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tej" href="/user/show/5074564-tej">Tej</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Tej by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/4027315-jasmeet">Jasmeet</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer249609480" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12863891865252259040">A story of a school history teacher, whose archaic name, Tertuliano Máximo Afonso, is a constant prick and an incessant irritant to him, only dealt with timid avoidance and shameful efforts at courage. This passage paints him well ;<br/><br/>"Tertuliano Máximo Afonso does not belong to that extraordinary group of people who can smile even when alone, his nature inclines him more to melancholy, to reverie, to an exaggerated awareness of the transience of life, to an incurable perplexity when faced by the g</span>
  <span id="freeText12863891865252259040" style="display:none">A story of a school history teacher, whose archaic name, Tertuliano Máximo Afonso, is a constant prick and an incessant irritant to him, only dealt with timid avoidance and shameful efforts at courage. This passage paints him well ;<br /><br />"Tertuliano Máximo Afonso does not belong to that extraordinary group of people who can smile even when alone, his nature inclines him more to melancholy, to reverie, to an exaggerated awareness of the transience of life, to an incurable perplexity when faced by the genuine Cretan labyrinths of human relationships." <br /><br /> It’s one of those books which you can love and hate at the same time. The theme is essentially bleak and unlike Dostoevskian ‘Double’ or Kafka’s ‘The Trial’, the canvas, rather than foraying purely into the dark entrails and black alleys, confines itself to incidents and events making use of vivid imagery and contemporary symbols albeit the core remains entrenched with existential questions harping on issues of one’s identity, it’s meaning, composition and scope.<br /><br />“he, Tertuliano Máximo Afonso, killed her, his moral weakness killed her, the will that made him blind to everything but revenge killed her, it was said that one of them, either the actor or the history teacher, was superfluous in this world, but you weren't, you weren't superluous, there is no duplicate of you to come and replace you at your mother's side, you were unique, just as every ordinary person is unique, truly unique.”<br /><br />“and in which it will be necessary to find some point of equilibrium between having been and continuing to be, it is doubtless comforting to have our consciousness tell us, I know who you are, but our own consciousness might start to doubt both us and its own words if it were to notice, all around, people asking each other the awkward question, Who's he.”<br /><br />To overcome his ‘depression’ a colleague suggests Tertuliano Maximo Afonso a movie and he accidentally chances upon the existence of his ‘double’, Antonio Claro, an actor in the movie, a head to toe exact duplicate who is neither a twin or related to him. His life is never the same from then on and he takes upon himself the task of solving this mystery quite literally to its bitter end. From being a tale of awe and surprise, assumptions and stupefactions, it turns into a tale of deceit and vengeance that nearing the end, has all the makings of a compelling thriller. It ends in tragedy with the death of Antonio Claro (who happens to carry the identity of Tertuliano Máximo Afonso, at the time of death) and Tertuliano Máximo Afonso’s fiancée Maria da Paz. Tertuliano Máximo Afonso now mistaken dead is forced to assume Antonio Claro’s identity and live with Helena, Antonio Claro’s wife. <br />What begins as an innocuous and natural predicament of Tertuliano Máximo Afonso ends in a sequence that bewilders and may even disgust. One can’t help thinking that the author has taken far too many liberties; it’s difficult to understand his intentions to a point that it appears obnoxiously absurd and downright trivial. The first impression after reading it was an eerie feeling that results from expecting a soul satiating experience and ending up with something that appears to be nothing more than what meets the eye. <br />‘Common sense’ is actually a character in the book that appears time to time to warn, to literally talk some sense into Tertuliano’s head who invariably ignores. He comes across as a stubborn, rigid and a possessed character who is best left alone in the matters concerning him, albeit to his own peril;<br /><br />"..the role of common sense in the history of your species has never gone beyond advising caution and chicken soup, especially in those cases where stupidity has already taken the floor and looks set to take the reins too,.."<br /><br />"..Why not, Because it's not healthy for the mind to live cheek by jowl with common sense, eating at the same table, sleeping in the same bed, taking it along to work, and asking its approval or permission before making a move, you've got to take a few risks of your own, Who do you mean, All of you, the human race,.."<br /><br />Almost all his relationships are cold and distant and that includes his own mother. Once when blanketed by the mist of reverie and sub consciousness, he imagines;<br /><br />",but the fact that boulder carried on back of the Amorite should have reminded him that he hadn't phoned his mother for nearly a week, even the most skilled interpreter of dreams would have been incapable of explaining to us, having excluded outright as insulting and ill intentioned, the easy interpretation that, deep down, and never daring to confess as much to himself, he thinks of his progenitor as a heavy burden."<br /> <br />Reflection into the work (complemented by the friend who suggested reading it), presented a possibility of a novel perspective into the nature of story. Like one of those ‘Christopher Nolan’ narratives, where the end is the beginning of confusing/intriguing questions, the answers lying in the deepest layers of mystery and surreal in the realm of individuals own understanding far removed from the author. <br /><br />“Gradually, like a cloud of steam flowing back to its place of origin, Tertuliano Máximo Afonso's terrified spirit returned to his exhausted mind, and when Helena asked, So what was this bad dream about, tell me, this confused man, this builder of labyrinths in which he himself is lost, who is lying now beside a woman who, although known to him in the sexual sense, is otherwise entirely unknown, spoke of a road that had ceased to have a beginning, as if his own steps as they were taken had devoured the very substances, whatever they might be, that give or lend duration to time and dimension to space,”<br /><br />What if, Saramago meant it all as a nightmare of Tertuliano Máximo Afonso, a schizophrenic, hallucinatory individual whose life is a conglomeration of indistinguishable layers of reality and fiction? The evidence to this is by no means conclusive but puts across just one of the many ways of interpretation. It appears that only when viewed laterally does this work shine up in all its brilliantly lit up details, else it falls too flat and is hard to believe. I ended up giving ‘one extra star’ only after realizing its amenability to a multitude of individualistic interpretations which is not obvious though. I understand there would be readers who will utterly disagree with any lateral quality of this book which appealed to me only after a long incubation period of deliberation. <br /><br />The work in fact is replete with Saramago signatures, his digressive and unconventional narrative style that leaves you literally out of breath at times because of the never ending sentences where two speakers are distinguished only by commas. At times the dialogues get all jumbled up and quite a few times one has to move backwards in the text flow in order to find the end that got lost in the maze of words and commas. If you are new to him, working your way through can be tedium indeed. But this very skein provides an apt background to the tone of his texts. You might even end up falling in love with this hallmark of his writing repertoire and rare acumen of weaving intricate tapestries out of characters who do not lead extraordinary lives and always seem within reach, yet their predicaments and travails are downright existential. It is easy to relate to them.<br /><br />"The human soul is a box out of which a clown is always ready to spring, making faces and sticking out his tongue, but there are times when that same clown merely peers at us over the edge of the box, and if he sees that, by chance, we are behaving in a just and honest fashion, he merely nods approvingly and disappears, thinking that we are not yet an entirely lost cause."<br /><br />This is an intriguing and unavoidable book which can best be read with an open mind and without great expectations. I ended up liking it although the appreciation was not immediate. The writing is downright brilliant and a compelling reason to reach for this one.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12863891865252259040" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1526437446" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1526437446" />
    <a title="Jigar Brahmbhatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3359025-jigar-brahmbhatt"><img alt="Jigar Brahmbhatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604042551p2/3359025.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1526437446?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jigar Brahmbhatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jigar Brahmbhatt" href="/user/show/3359025-jigar-brahmbhatt">Jigar Brahmbhatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099461654" href="/book/show/826799.The_Double">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1526437446" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9648044303335446882">I want to start by saying that I wanted to enjoy this novel, I really did. Look at the cover design! How can one not be thrilled by a premise like this? An ordinary guy (a history professor in our case) goes about his mundane life day in day out without so much as a single moment of excitement... and bam! He sees his double in a movie. <br/><br/>But here is what is off-putting. This is my first Saramago novel, and I may be blinded by my quirky tastes in judging the Nobel laureate. But there is a way of sh</span>
  <span id="freeText9648044303335446882" style="display:none">I want to start by saying that I wanted to enjoy this novel, I really did. Look at the cover design! How can one not be thrilled by a premise like this? An ordinary guy (a history professor in our case) goes about his mundane life day in day out without so much as a single moment of excitement... and bam! He sees his double in a movie. <br /><br />But here is what is off-putting. This is my first Saramago novel, and I may be blinded by my quirky tastes in judging the Nobel laureate. But there is a way of showing movement in the narrative and every writer has their own way of doing it. Here's the scene: the protagonist has to go to a video rental shop to rent the films that interest him. Simple!<br /><br />How Saramago writes the scene: There are languages in the world that call Wednesday, for example, mercredi, miercoles, mercoledi or querta-feira, that call Thursday jeudi, jueves, or quanta-fiera, and as for Friday, if we had not taken overt precaution of protecting its name, there would even be people out there who would start calling it Freitag. It may yet happen, all in good time, its moment may come. Having clarified this point, having agreed that today is Friday, having mentioned that the history teacher will have classes today only in the afternoon, and having noted that tomorrow, Saturday, samedi, sabado, sabato, there will be no classes, that we are therefore on the eve of the weekend, but, above all, because one should never put off till tomorrow what one can do today, it is clear that [our protagonist] is quite right to go to the video rental shop this morning so that he can rent the remaining films that interest him.<br /><br />PHEW!!!<br /><br />The names of the days in different languages lend richness to the text, but notice how obvious details are stretched as if just to increase the word count. And notice how the writer says that the protagonist is "quite right" to go to the video rental shop. This made me laugh out loud. What does it mean when a writer provides an uncalled-for justification? The word "pompous" immediately came into my mind but then I had a fight it out. A Hemingway or a Salinger would have got it over with in just one line: "It was a Friday. He was free in the afternoon."<br /><br />Now I am not in favor of sparseness in prose. Saul Bellow was a master of urgent, winding prose, and he even attacked Hemingway's style by saying: "Do you have emotions? Strangle them." But I am sure Bellow never read the above para by Saramago. I believe a writer has to draw a line on how far he wants to be carried away in his own style. Short sentences won't necessarily make you a pulp writer, and long, ever-going ones won't make you a James Joyce. <br /><br />It is a very interesting story, made richer by Saramago's tangential narrative style, his inquiries on philosophy and history, but at the same time made dull by sentences after sentences of the kind I have quoted above. The Borgesian idea of two bodies competing to share a single ontological space is a classic one, and how I would have loved it if someone like Paul Auster had conceived and executed it! This is more a gut feeling of this reader than a critique. <br /><br />Because it is sad when the only motive you have of finishing the book is to make up for the good 400 bucks you have spent on it! </span>
  <a data-text-id="9648044303335446882" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_86427020" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/86427020" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/86427020?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099461654" href="/book/show/826799.The_Double">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Tata J</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Must Read Books Before You Die
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-non-core">1001-non-core</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer86427020" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1445949910901162759">My 2nd book by the 1998 Nobel Prize for Literature awardee, Jose de Sousa Saramago. I am still impressed.<br/><br/>With <i>Blindness</i> as the first, I can now say that Mr. Saramago has a distinctive writing style: he uses long sentences, at times more than a page long. He also uses periods sparingly, choosing instead a loose flow of clauses joined by commas. Many of his paragraphs extend for pages without pausing for dialog, which Mr. Saramago chooses not to delimit by quotation marks; when the speaker changes</span>
  <span id="freeText1445949910901162759" style="display:none">My 2nd book by the 1998 Nobel Prize for Literature awardee, Jose de Sousa Saramago. I am still impressed.<br /><br />With <i>Blindness</i> as the first, I can now say that Mr. Saramago has a distinctive writing style: he uses long sentences, at times more than a page long. He also uses periods sparingly, choosing instead a loose flow of clauses joined by commas. Many of his paragraphs extend for pages without pausing for dialog, which Mr. Saramago chooses not to delimit by quotation marks; when the speaker changes, Saramago capitalizes the first letter of the new speaker's clause.<br /><br />This style makes reading his novel quite difficult and if you are not used to it, you might say that he has excess unnecessary verbiage. However, I think that there is where the beauty of reading is. When you read a book with straight no-fuss storytelling style (your famous bestsellers like John Grishman or Dean Koontz easily come to my mind), <i>komiks na komiks ang dating.</i> But if once in a while you encounter distinctive writing styles like Nabokov's, G. G. Marquez's or even Khaled Hosseini's, you will be surprised to find out that those styles not only can work too but work with magic and it offers variety.<br /><br />This novel, <i>The Double</i> is distinctively Saramago's. It is not an easy read. However, the ending is truly amazing. I will not spoil the fun here because I think it what makes this novel really worth reading. I just did not expect it to end that way. Like <i>The Prince and the Pauper</i> pulverized, re-shaped, twisted, spun and rebuilt by a genius to make a literary masterpiece of the 21st century. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1445949910901162759" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText6809752942787581754" style="display:none">What would you do if you found out that you have an identical duplicate living in the same city? Tertuliano Maximo Afonso cannot help himself... he becomes obsessed with finding this other person and meeting him. Was it a good idea? Well, the common sense (that has conversations with Maximo throughout the book) has substantiated doubts...It's just that humans are not always prone to listening to common sense. <br /><br />I have to admit that I didn't connect with the book straight away. It took me a while to get used to the writing style (long sentences, pages of uninterrupted text, a narrator that's commenting on things that could have been...) but it grows on you. It's a slow read that picks up the pace towards the end. For me, the ending of the book is always important and this time the author really brought it home. The last few chapters quite surprised me and I have a feeling I won't forget this book very soon.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13452690159342474199">It will take me some time to digest this book. I have just closed it. I missed Saramagos writing style, which is not everybody's thing (just read the 1-2 star reviews). His style sucks you in, it is hard to stop reading.<br/>This story belongs to those "dystopy" stories Saramago used to write, those "What if..."s. We are not offered a logical scientific explanation why there are two persons 100% alike, but this wasn't the scope of the story. I think it was all about how do you deal with this type of </span>
  <span id="freeText13452690159342474199" style="display:none">It will take me some time to digest this book. I have just closed it. I missed Saramagos writing style, which is not everybody's thing (just read the 1-2 star reviews). His style sucks you in, it is hard to stop reading.<br />This story belongs to those "dystopy" stories Saramago used to write, those "What if..."s. We are not offered a logical scientific explanation why there are two persons 100% alike, but this wasn't the scope of the story. I think it was all about how do you deal with this type of situation.<br />The psychology is perfect, the logical actions of the characters are, well, logical. <br />And then there is the end... quantum physics? paralel universes? As I said, I still have to digest this book. But it is a fantastic and thrilling read, highly recommended.<br />PS - I wonder if there is some sort of double identity folclore in the history of mesopotamian people...<br />PS - only the "house", the "shell" was identical. There were completely different persons. So, if everything anatomically and biochemically and neurophysiologically is the same, how come they think and act completely different? Because the society shapes us, every second means another second of experience which modifies one's self. do we exist in this same form in a parallel universe which we dont see?????<br />Anyways...still digesting...<br />PS - the only thing that intrigues me is the scar they both had above the knee. Scar implies an accident, so external forces, has nothing to do with the DNA anymore...The scar theme is repeated so many times, i am sure a logical person like Saramago had something in his mind about it...if you find the answer, do tell me !!!</span>

<div id="review_823058588" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/823058588" />
    <a title="Deepthi Terenz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16718539-deepthi-terenz"><img alt="Deepthi Terenz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503062620p2/16718539.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/823058588?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deepthi Terenz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deepthi Terenz" href="/user/show/16718539-deepthi-terenz">Deepthi Terenz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16718539-deepthi-terenz?shelf=must-read">must-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16718539-deepthi-terenz?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer823058588" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13135396410247274496">Saramago tells the story of the History professor Tertuliano Máximo Afonso who one day, while viewing a banal video a colleague has recommended, discovers that one of the minor actors in it is his identical. Tertuliano becomes obsessed with the idea of meeting the person he takes to be his double and, after viewing dozens of other films, manages to discover the actor's name, Antonio Claro, and track him down. This book was a little difficult to read as Saramago proved himself to be the master of</span>
  <span id="freeText13135396410247274496" style="display:none">Saramago tells the story of the History professor Tertuliano Máximo Afonso who one day, while viewing a banal video a colleague has recommended, discovers that one of the minor actors in it is his identical. Tertuliano becomes obsessed with the idea of meeting the person he takes to be his double and, after viewing dozens of other films, manages to discover the actor's name, Antonio Claro, and track him down. This book was a little difficult to read as Saramago proved himself to be the master of long sentences with The Double. Saramago also doesn’t use quotation marks to denote when people are speaking, and he doesn’t break up conversations aside from inserting commas in between each person’s statements. This made the book pretty heavy. But I’m glad I stuck with it, because the ending of this book was completely unexpected. I really enjoyed the storyline and I found myself doing a lot of thinking between reading sessions. I don’t know what I would do in Tertuliano’s situation. I found the book very enjoyable once I got used to Saramago’s choice of writing style. I was sucked in by the story and found the idea of two random, identical people in the world pretty interesting. Only thing disappointing for me is that Saramago appears less willing to explore more about all the characters, he created.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13135396410247274496" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2497311648" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2497311648" />
    <a title="Lemar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5172572-lemar"><img alt="Lemar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595783002p2/5172572.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2497311648?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lemar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lemar" href="/user/show/5172572-lemar">Lemar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2497311648" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7238063306403292476">Wisdom lurks in this novel. In fact Common Sense is actually a minor character (a bit huffy too about his small role) saying things like,<br/><br/>“What will be, will be, Oh i know that’s philosophy, it’s what people call predestination, fatalism, fate, but what it really means is that, as usual, you’ll do whatever you choose to do”<br/><br/>So, yes Common Sense and our protagonist, (saddled with the name Tertuliano)are often at odds, which endears him to us. Samarago, as beautifully translated by Margaret Jill Cos</span>
  <span id="freeText7238063306403292476" style="display:none">Wisdom lurks in this novel. In fact Common Sense is actually a minor character (a bit huffy too about his small role) saying things like,<br /><br />“What will be, will be, Oh i know that’s philosophy, it’s what people call predestination, fatalism, fate, but what it really means is that, as usual, you’ll do whatever you choose to do”<br /><br />So, yes Common Sense and our protagonist, (saddled with the name Tertuliano)are often at odds, which endears him to us. Samarago, as beautifully translated by Margaret Jill Costa, creates a labyrinth of mirrors that José Luis Borges would appreciate. Questions of identity and how we appear to others are rendered with amazing subtlety. <br /><br />“we pay attention to the multiple scintillations of the subgestures that follow behind a gesture like the cosmic dust in the tail of a comet”<br /><br />Samarago, like great writers through the ages, describes attitudes that are suddenly in the spotlight at future times. In our times, as we languish under a president who challenges verities of truth and science, are these observations,<br /><br />“Being a man should never be an impediment to behaving like a gentleman”<br /><br />“People gather together under an opinion as if it were an umbrella.”<br /><br />And, finally, one for all eras, <br />“Please forgive the repetition, which we make in the name of the right we all have to say over and over where the pain is.”<br /></span>
